and two times this error so the errors are kind of combining to give you the error in the product alright so I think this makes it very clear that sort of an interval arithmetic point of view can help precise way by talking about intervals so let's talk about the laws of arithmetic for intervals with natural numbers there are the commutative laws the I plus J equals J plus I and that I times J # 086 - Newton's method and algebraic curves ｜ Real numbers and limits Math Foundations 86 ｜ N J Wildberger [tFLCNKWe9oI].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day I'm Norman Wildberger today's video is the last video in the first part of this course in the foundations of mathematics where we're having a look at how to set up how we can extend Newton's method to find approximate intersections of algebraic curves and we're going to look at one particular example so there's an underlying question here well what exactly is an algebraic curve and for us the answer is that it's basically a bi poly number P say depending on alpha and beta again we'll have a look at two particular algebraic curves the firma curve which looks like this perhaps x cubed plus y cubed equals one is the traditional Cartesian equation for it studied by firma very important curve and the second curve is the lemniscate of Bernoulli an even more important curve investigated by Jacob Bernoulli looks like this and it has equation x squared plus y squared all squared equals 2x squared minus y squared again in Cartesian coordinates now you can see from these two pictures that the red Vermont curve and the blue lemniscate meet at these two points now those are the only two points of where they meet and so a natural question is well can we find out where those two points are algebraically it means solving these two equations simultaneously which is rather a challenging thing to do and in fact you can't do it over the rational numbers in fact over the rational numbers the firma curve itself is already very difficult to get ahold of in this form because firma himself proved that the only irrational points on this curve are the two places where it meets the x and y axis at 1 0 and 0 1 there are no other rational numbers x and y that satisfy that equation but nevertheless we'll see that there is a approximate version of this question so we can find approximate points here and here that lie approximately on this curve in a sense that they almost satisfy this equation and they lie approximately on this curve in the sense that they almost satisfy this equation and that's what we're going to be after that's what Newton's method allows us to find it allows us to find an approximate solution to an equation which in practice is all that we can expect and hope for and all that we often need so let's start by having a look at the firma curve x cubed plus y cubed equals 1 the associated by pali number is this one here minus 1 plus alpha cubed plus beta cubed where alpha is playing the role of x and beta is playing the role of y so this fits into our poly number framework which is a very explicit and computationally simple way of dealing with polynomials now when we talked about by Pauli numbers like this sometime ago I told you that there were a number of different interpretations of such a poly number there are different ways of visualizing or imagining what this expression represents and I explained that we have to sometimes have a flexible attitude as to what interpretation we're going to be using so one interpretation of such a Pauline of is interpretation a where we interpret it as a curve P of X y equals zero so in other words we're setting the equation or expression equal to zero and looking to see where we can have some solutions in the plane that's where we get the familiar equation x cubed plus y cubed equals 1 from and that's where this picture comes from this is a curve in the plane at least we can see it and what we're really graphing here are not points that exactly satisfy this equation but points which approximately satisfy this equation so this is a picture of rational numbers all kinds of rational numbers x and y such that x cubed plus y cubed is pretty close to 1 and pretty close is variable depending on your computer program depending on the resolution that you want to obtain it's a curve it's a two-dimensional picture then there's also interpretation B which is different we take the expression now and we create from it a surface by essentially looking at this equation by introducing a third variable Z and thinking about the surface Z equals P of XY so for any two values X and y we can compute P of XY that's a number and then that's going to give us the height above the point XY that the surface lies on so here's our three-dimensional picture at least a two-dimensional representation of it the x-direction coming out at us the y-direction in the board the z direction in the board so the XY plane that was visualized over here in this way is now visualized lying horizontally with the x axis coming out at us here and the y axis there so # 088 - The decline of rigour in modern mathematics ｜ Real numbers and limits Math Foundations 88 [Nnp4qZX9H1U].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day I'm Norman Wildberger this is the second video in the second part of the series on the foundations of mathematics and we're turning our attention to modern pure mathematics and critiquing it in fact we're putting mathematics on trial and I have a public prosecutor today we're going to look at the notion of rigor in mathematics and I'm going to start to try to explain why rigor has been decreasing in fact over the last few hundred years and notably in the last century this is rather contrary to the public perception along the way we're going to have a look at the notion of proof and how it figures in the story so first of all we might start by saying what is rigor in mathematics well one online dictionary defines it this way that rigor is logical validity or accuracy and that's a pretty good initial stab at it historically the notion of rigor has been intimately connected with Euclid's deductive framework as set out in his book the elements which was a kind of Bible or road map for logical mathematical presentation for several thousand years so the model that Euclid set up and I've talked earlier in this series about Euclid so if you haven't watched those videos you can go back and have a look the basic framework is he starts with definitions and obvious facts and the obvious facts are grouped into postulates and axioms and then with those as the preparation then he starts with theorems which are statements of facts and proofs so he states something and then he proves it from the original obvious facts and the definitions and then there's an X theorem and the proof of that and the next theorem a proof of that and so on there's a logical succession starting with the simpler theorems and working the way slowly towards more and more complicated theorems and the idea is that you're allowed to use the previous theorems that you've already proven in the proof of any subsequent theorem so there's a very important framework that you could set up and for most people this is still what rigor means and it's quite a reasonable definition or approach to what rigor is however in the last hundred years or so another notion of rigor has emerged so often when analysts talk about rigor these days they are referring not so much to Euclid's framework but rather to a framework that was set up in the latter part of the 19th century when mathematicians started investigating the foundations for analysis and major names associated to that mikoshi Dedekind wire straws and there's a particular kind of formulation which often is associated with the epsilon and delta limit notions and this framework replaced earlier usage of infinitesimals which had been considered dubious by some people and they'll also replace to some extent the algebraic approach that earlier generations had adopted towards analysis namely particularly Euler and LaGrange and then helped on by Cantor's theory of infinite sets this this sort of approach became in the 20th century sort of the standard dogma for analysis and to this day when an analyst refers to rigor often they are making some reference to this framework now well there were some very important ideas here there were also some rather dubious ideas as we are going to see later on so for me it's not at all obvious that this approach should be associated with the term rigor in fact I'm going to argue something of the opposite as we go on the notion of rigor is so important in mathematics that I think we need to flesh this out a little bit more so here are some characteristics of rigorous thinking which I think are particularly valuable first of all we need careful and precise initial definitions the definitions that we make we must be very careful with them not too arbitrary they must be chosen just the right way and they must be completely precise we should aim for a minimum of assumptions when we're starting a theory we may very well have some assumptions we may for example assume some prior theories that have already been created that's fine but we should first of all be very clear about what our assumptions are and we should try to aim for a minimum of assumptions and assumptions that we do make ought to be pretty reasonable ones the step by step nature of rigorous mathematics where we're going one step at a time and each step is logical and clear that's very important it's easy to make mistakes even when you're trying hard to be rigorous when you're trying hard to be careful and so we need to check our work constantly we need to be cross-referencing things and looking at things from different directions to make sure that we haven't made a mistake so we must be very careful in mathematics to avoid those little mistakes or not so little mistakes that can creep into our theories and one way of keeping them at bay is to have a thorough working out of examples if we work through examples we can check our theories against the examples and there has to be some kind of correspondence there and if there isn't well that's meaning that we need to go back and check something another good way of checking thing is to try to make lots of computations and make sure that we do them carefully so we check our theoretical results against explicit computations that we've done carefully so the emphasis is on care on being methodical on being thorough on being logical and we have to make sure that we're not leaving any gaps if there's a little logical gap somewhere there could be an error contained in that gap could wreck the entire argument so generally we need to have a critical and skeptical approach to things we don't just assume things because people come and say oh look I have proved a nice new result we say AHA that looks nice now you show me the proof convince me that it's true and I'm going to look at it skeptically I'm going to be critical so as I've mentioned several times before when we're talking about mathematics and rigor in mathematics the importance of Euclid's elements can't be overemphasized up to the beginning of the 20th century the primary model for correct mathematical rigor was Euclid's elements so studying mathematics was almost synonymous with learning geometry from Euclid those two terms were pretty close to meaning the same thing for many generations of students and mathematicians however in the early 1900's the situation started to change Euclid was cut back people started questioning the need to go through Euclid so carefully so first they started cutting back the amount of Euclid that was presented and then gradually other people may text that replaced Euclid at least partially and then eventually Euclid was slowly eliminated altogether so that today Euclid is almost nowhere taught it's a dramatic departure from the historical record one that we need to be aware of as a consequence of that our attitudes towards mathematics education have changed in a very important way so the basic model has changed from a logical succession of theorems and proofs we essentially Euclid's model to a more flexible assortment of facts and methods these days students are not so much given a linear structure where one thing follows from the other and you need to do things in the right order that was Euclid's model these days students are rather given an assortment of facts and the facts are somewhat flexible you can interchange the order of them without too much problem the facts are often only thinly supported by proofs there might be a little bit of explanation or some examples to illustrate a fact to give the students some confidence in the fact but the idea that facts have to be rigorously demonstrated that is an idea from yesteryear sadly and instead of proofs the emphasis has been shifting towards methods where students now learn how to do things how to find the eigenvalues of a matrix how to find the derivative of this how to solve this linear system of equations how to factor that polynomial this is considered the main thing that students have to learn instead of how do you prove that theorem how do you prove that theorem how do you go from there to there how do you solve that problem very different emphasis so this explains a lot the shift in attitudes to rigor that has been experienced in the 20th century and it's been a very market shift in our attitudes towards rigor not surprisingly the students that have come through this system are not so concerned with proofs and so this idea of rigor of having to formally substantiate facts it doesn't weigh so heavily upon them and mathematicians having gone through that system too naturally a little bit of that rubs off on us even if in higher years we try to transcend it as a result today in 2012 there are very few college or university courses which lay out a complete self-contained logical presentation of a given mathematical subject such a thing is different ways that I've pointed out and the first way that we're going to look at starting today is the idea of a real number or irrational number as an infinite decimal we already saw that an ordinary rational number like 13 over 8 can be represented as a decimal number like this under certain conditions when the denominator had a certain form then we could actually divide and get a finite repeating decimal where you have a period like this that gets repeated over and over again both of these expressions were in the context of finite expressions because well we can write them all down in a finite way using this / bar notation the current understanding of decimals traces back to an interesting Flemish mathematician Simon Stevin who lived in this period 1548 to 1620 that he was really the first person to advocate the modern theory of real numbers as infinite decimals Steffen contributed to mathematics in a number of other ways he investigated the tides relationship with the moon he was an engineer and rather practical person and he for example created something called a land yacht which is a sort of a boat on wheels with sails that quite fast along the beach which was the kind of thing that we're after this is what it looks like we've got a decimal point we've got a finite number of digit to the left we have an infinite number of digits to the right and perhaps there is a plus or minus sign in front the question is what exactly are we talking about here and there are essentially these days three different answers three very important different directions of going in terms of understanding what is an infinite decimal here are the three different rows that we can go down the first which is the most popular and the most well accepted these days is the approach what I call infinite choice it's also sometimes called quasi combinatorial all right so in this approach we take the following position that an infinite decimal like this is first of all a finite choice of sign so you either choose the plus or the minus and if you choose the plus you don't actually have to write it down but if you choose the - you have to write it down then you have to choose a number K and you have to choose digits B 0 B 1 up to be K now the digits are always numbers from 0 to 9 okay so you chosen the sign you chosen K you chosen a certain number of digits to the left of the decimal point and now you need to choose an infinite number of digits a 1 a 2 a 3 and so on to the right of the decimal point and these digits can each be chosen arbitrarily and independently so you choose digit a 1 make up some number some digit between 0 &amp; 9 let's say 5 and then you choose a - say 3 and then you choose a 3 say 7 and then a 4 maybe that's 7 as well and you keep going there doesn't have to be a pattern to your choices there can be a pattern but if there is a pattern you can keep it to your of ultimately what's required is the infinite string of choices that you're making and that is what an infinite decimal is then there's a second position the second position we might call the algorithmic or the constructive position it's very much like the first one except for the crucial part of addressing the digits to the right of the decimal point with this algorithmic or constructive point of view we insist that the digits a 1 a 2 and a 3 have to be determined by a finite algorithm there has to be a finite algorithm or procedure or you can think of a formula you can think of a computer program some finite way of specifying how these digits are generated so we don't have to actually have the digits themselves all in a row we just need this finite piece of paper that tells us how to generate the digits that's a rather different approach to what the infinite decimal actually is then there's a third position which we might call the finite issed or the rational position in this position infinite decimals are not required and they are deemed illogical so this position asserts that both these positions are unfounded mathematically that both of these positions are problematic and it says in fact guys we do not need infinite decimals in either of these forms right so I think you probably can guess which position I am going to adopt but before I do that we're going to have to have a very good look at both of these two positions decimal what we need to do is we need to say what all the digits are in the right order so this is one five point one four eight eight zero two and then we keep on going maybe there's a six seven nine and so on so this specification of the digits is very much in keeping with what we do with finite decimals the second advantage is that the representation is almost unique there's pretty well only one way of specifying an infinite choice into the decimal so equality between infinite decimals is easy to detect there is an important qualification though that we agree that something like three point seven nine nine nine 9 where the nines keep on going is equal to three point eight zero zero zero zero zero where the zeros keep on going so that's the one place where infinite decimal has an ambiguous representation there are two ways of representing it and that's of course exactly when it's a finite decimal of this kind so it's exactly the finite decimals sitting inside the infinite decimals which have these ambiguous aspects to it but otherwise the representation is unique and now we come to what's perhaps the most important advantage in this infinite choice approach the advantage is that this idea or concept turns a process into a number let me try to explain what I mean by that so in certain situations we have problems which we cannot exactly solve but we can move towards a solution and we've seen that with regard to Newton's method so for example suppose we have this cubic equation represented by that graph and suppose that we're interested in finding it's zero say that the biggest zero that one that's right there so if you look back a few videos into my videos that have Newton's method in their title you'll see that we've outlined a rather explicit procedure that allows us to generate rational approximations to a number that satisfies this equation we may not in fact ever be able to get a number that exactly satisfies this equation but we can generate numbers step by step that more and more closely solve this equation geometrically we may start with a value of x say r1 and then using Newton's method we generate r2 and then r3 and we can keep going generating more and more rational members that are getting closer and closer to having the property that they're both on the x-axis and on this curve so we get successive approximations R 1 R 2 R 3 and so on all rational so when we convert these numbers into decimal form giving us repeating decimals then we notice that the initial strings of these decimals start agreeing the difference between R 1 and R 2 and R 3 becomes manifested further and further along in the decimal representations so in other words as we keep going in the process we are essentially generating more and more digits in this infinite decimal and what we are able to do with this idea is to turn this process into a number the whole process of Newton's method gets encoded in the single decimal admittedly it does go on forever it has an infinite amount of information in it in some sense and that number can then be used according to this concept as a number we can do arithmetic with this number we can see where it is we can compare it with other numbers and so on alright so a process has been turned into a number this is an aspect of this which has the widest ramifications and seems to convey a lot of benefits and two very important results which are consequences of what we said on the previous page are these two the intermediate value theorem and the fundamental theorem of algebra they are both results that depend on this notion of infinite choice decimal number so it may be a little while since you've done calculus or perhaps you haven't calculus so we don't need to know exactly what the intermediate value theorem says but it's roughly that if it looks like there is a solution then there is a solution somewhat more precisely if you have a continuous function f of X with here its graph and I won't exactly define what this means but you can think of it as something that you can draw on the page without lifting your pen then if you have some points a and B on the x axis so that the functions value at a is less than zero and the functions value at B is bigger than zero then there's going to be some number C in between a and B that has the property that F of C is zero in other words there's a zero to the function somewhere between a and B so this result is intimately connected with this infinite process business generating a number so we can get at such a thing by successively bracketing so you can imagine sort of bracketing instead of choosing a and B we choose a1 and b1 which are closer still less than zero still bigger than zero on both sides if we keep moving closer and closer and closer we can generate this sequence of numbers and then the idea of an infinite choice infinite decimal allows us to then deduce that there is actually a number that has this limiting property that we want so the intermediate value theorem is intimately connected and dependent on this idea of an infinite decimal the fundamental theorem of algebra let me say it in the 18th century way any polynomial with real coefficients that is with coefficients which are these real numbers can be factored into linear and quadratic factors remarkable and highly non-obvious theorem which again ultimately rests on this this infinite process into a number formulation that that is encoded in the infinite choice approach to decimals so those two theorems that I mentioned are just the start of a whole range of theorems in analysis which ultimately come back to the the notion of an infinite decimal you need the notion of a real number to be able to establish these theorems as they are usually stated okay we will see later on that there's often alternate ways of stating the same theorems which does not require infinite decimals okay so it's not the case that these theorems absolutely require this notion but the current approach definitely does build on infinite decimals as infinite choice creations another advantage of the infinite choice approach is that the truncation of an infinite decimal yields our rational number approximation so for example that infinite decimal that we created from the cubic equation alpha equals four point six nine six three nine two and so on an applied mathematician can come along and say oh look we're just going to chop this off and ignore all these other infinite amount of tail digits there but to an applied mathematician these extra digits just amount to more and more delicate information regarding very very small small changes in position the further we go here the more irrelevant these digits are to an applied mathematician and I'm talking about physicist or chemist or computer scientist here possibly also so this is very useful that we can just take one of these abstract theoretical pure mathematical ideas number so that's been a list of advantages in thinking about a real number as an infinite decimal involving an infinite number of choices of all the digits one at a time however there are some strong # 092 - Difficulties with real numbers as infinite decimals (II) ｜ Real numbers + limits Math Foundations 92 [ScLgc_98XxM].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day. I'm Norman Wildberger. We are looking at the difficulties with establishing real numbers as infinite decimals. These are very serious difficulties, which the professor these days is doing their best to avoid acknowledging, and there's a good reason for it because the price to be paid, as we'll see, is quite significant, or at least it appears to be. However, we'll see that there are great opportunities as well by acknowledging the difficulties and moving beyond them. Last time, we talked about infinite decimals in terms of infinite choice sequences—the idea that it was possible to prescribe decimals one at a time, arbitrarily and independently, forever and ever. We basically explained very clearly, I hope, the difficulties with that position. Today, we're going to look at another approach to infinite decimals: the algorithmic, constructive, or computational approach. In this approach, we think of an infinite decimal as being expressed by an algorithm, so the sequence of digits that we're trying to get at can be captured by an algorithm, a procedure, a function, a computer program, or some equivalent method. So we really have these three positions: the infinite choice position, the algorithmic, constructive, or computational position, and then a third position, which is really the only one left to us once we acknowledge the difficulty or impossibility of the first two positions. That third position is a finitist or rational position, which is that infinite decimals are illogical and unnecessary. Ultimately, this is the position that we are going to adopt, and I am going to try to show you that mathematics is not impoverished by this position but actually unfolds before us in a brand new and exciting way. Alright, today we're going to look at the algorithmic, constructive, or computational approach to what is an infinite decimal, and we're going to look at the pros and cons. Let me start by saying that I have a lot of sympathy for this approach to infinite decimals. I think this is a very reasonable thing to try to do—to try to look at an infinite decimal like 3.1415926, and so on, with digits that go on and on forever, by approaching that from the point of view that says, "Well, we need an algorithm that describes the digits, and we'll think of the infinite decimal as really being encoded by the algorithm." The beauty of that is that the algorithm is a finite object, and so we can work with the infinite decimal as represented by this finite object. I think that's a very sensible, reasonable, and natural thing to try to do. The only problem with it is that it's too difficult to do, and ultimately, we can't do it in the year 2012. Alright, so what do we mean, first of all, by an algorithm? Clearly, we need to think about what that word means. So intuitively, we might say that it's a finite list of well-defined instructions that give us a step-by-step procedure for a calculation. Often, the calculation that we want to do is of a function. This is an English-language definition, something that you might find in a dictionary. I don't think at all that it's clear enough and precise enough for mathematical usage, but at least it gives us an initial orientation to what an algorithm is. Those of you who are familiar with computers can, if you like, think of it as being essentially a computer program. So what we have here, for example, is a piece of paper or a file on your hard drive with instructions: instruction number one, instruction number two, perhaps down to instruction number 15. These instructions allow us, for example, to calculate a function to capture this number pi. The function naturally might input the place of the decimal and output the decimal value, so F of 1 equals 1, F of 2 equals 4, F of 3 equals 1, and F of 0 equals 3, let's say, F of 7 equals 6, and so on. So the idea is, you want to know what is the 1, 2, 3, 4, 5—the 5th decimal place. So you input the number 5, perform the operations on the number 5, eventually you get an output, and hopefully, that output is the digit 9. And that's how we can specify the entire infinite decimal by a very finite, cut-and-dried procedure. It's a very attractive idea, an attractive idea which ultimately doesn't work. As we go down this route, it's clear that there are some significant challenges ahead of us. First of all, we are going to need an underlying theory or understanding of algorithms. We're going to have to know a lot about algorithms: what they are, how they work, how they're implemented. We need a theory of algorithms. Secondly, we're going to have to acknowledge that conceptually, this framework for establishing a number system is much, much more complicated than for rational numbers. Rational numbers—1/6 plus 2/7 equals 19 over 42—what a rational number is, is pretty simple. It's a pair of integers; how we add them is reasonably simple; how we multiply them is reasonably simple. Here, what we're going to be dealing with is algorithms, computer programs, and we're going to have to deal with the challenge of how you add them, how you multiply them, how you check the laws of arithmetic. It's a big challenge. We'll probably need help from computer science because we're talking about algorithms. The computer scientists know all about algorithms. They're the ones who work with these things; they have very elaborate experience and theories for them. However, within mathematics, there's also the theory of computation. Mathematicians also study computation. So there's something called the lambda calculus, there are things called Turing machines, there's something called automata theory, and other things as well. And the question is, will we need any of these theories in order to set up our approach to infinite decimals as algorithms? Alright, so what are the pros and cons of trying to look at infinite decimals in terms of algorithms? Well, there are lots of advantages; there are lots of good reasons for trying to do this. The first one, perhaps, is that this general orientation agrees with the historical point of view. Historically, the infinite decimals that people dealt with were given by procedures, by algorithms, by rules that generated digits one at a time. For example, the square root algorithm, which we've talked about several points in this series, going back to the Babylonians. The Hindus had such an algorithm, the Greeks, the Arabs, and then Newton for square root of 2, square root of 5, and so on. These are procedures that work step by step, and they allow us to get closer and closer to rational approximations, which can be converted into further and further initial strings of a decimal. There's also a cube root algorithm, which we haven't talked about, but it's a more sophisticated version of a square root algorithm. In fact, it was studied by ancient Indian mathematician Aryabhata around 500 AD, and of course, it's also accessible by Newton's method. And then we must mention pi, which plays a very big role in the history of people's fascination with infinite decimals. So pi has been tackled going back to Archimedes, who initiated an essentially algorithmic process for approximating pi. But the first, perhaps modern, formula goes back to an Indian mathematician Madhava from Kerala in southern India in the 1300s, who came up with this remarkable formula that pi is equal to the square root of 12 times [1 minus 1 over 3 times 3 plus 1 over 5 times 3 squared minus 1 over 7 times 3 cubed, and so on]. So each one of these terms is getting smaller because the denominators are getting bigger, keep multiplying by the next odd number and then by powers of 3. So we're adding and subtracting smaller and smaller quantities, and if you go to a certain number of terms, then you can compute a relatively accurate approximation to pi provided you also know how to take a square root of 2. And Madhava, in fact, was able to calculate pi to this number of decimal places using 21 terms of this series. Now, this is an algorithmic approach. You have a very cut-and-dried formula. You can go as far as you want in this formula. You can compute something; you get an initial part of the decimal. So in principle, you can keep going, keep going, getting more and more digits this way. So the decimal is encoded by this formula, which we could think of these days as also a procedure or computer program if we like. And historically, there have been many, many other formulas for pi. In my next video, in fact, we are going to have a good look at pi and some of the many formulas that people have come up with that essentially give us algorithms for computing pi. Alright, so historically, this point of view corresponds to what people used to think. Stevin, when he introduced real numbers in the 17th century, would have been thinking about these infinite decimals in this algorithmic fashion because the main examples that he would have had, like roots and pi, were of that form. Also, I suppose repeating decimals, which are also a special and very simple example of an algorithm that generates digits. Another advantage to this algorithmic approach, if we can make it work, is that it allows evaluation of so-called infinite series. These are very important constructions in analysis, and the real numbers, as we currently have them, allow us—or seem to allow us—to evaluate these things. For example, consider this infinite series. So it's an infinite series in the sense that we're adding more and more terms, but the terms that we're adding are getting smaller and smaller, so we're converging to something, we think, and that something that we're converging to is a real number. Now, this series here, the terms are actually forming a very nice and important pattern. Here we have 1 over 2, and then 1 over 2 times 3, and then 2 times 3 times 4, 2 times 3 times 4 times 5, and the denominators—so these numbers are called factorials. So we can write this summation more compactly using this summation sign introduced by Euler. So we're summing from N equals 0 to infinity 1 over N factorial. 1 over 0 factorial is 1 by definition because 0 factorial is defined to be 1. Then there's 1 over 1 factorial, 1 over 2 factorial, 1 over 3 factorial, 1 over 4 factorial, and so on. So how do we approach such an infinite series? Through the idea of partial sums, where we go from N equals 0 to some fixed natural number K. So in other words, we add up all the terms up to a certain amount and compute what we've got. So in this case, the first partial sum is just that first term, which is 1. The second partial sum, the first two terms, that's 2. Then the next one will be 5/2. As a decimal, 2.5. And then when we add this term here, we get 8/3, which is 2.6 repeated. Then we add this one on top of it, and we get 65/24, which turns out to be 2.7083 repeated. Then s5 is 163/60—that's 2.716 repeated. s6 is this, s7 is this, and s8 is this, starting to get a little bit more complicated here. The repetition only kicks in at this level here. Now, if we look at all these partial sums, we see that they are sort of converging to something in the sense that the first terms start to stabilize after a while. So here we get 2.718, and here's another 2.718, 2.718. All the further terms are going to have 2.718 in them. So as we keep going and keep going, we end up generating more and more digits. So this is really an algorithm; it's really a step-by-step procedure that can be used to create an infinite decimal step by step, slowly, slowly, more and more. Here is the limiting value. The limiting value is a very important number called e—that's the base of natural logarithms. It plays a very important role in mathematics. It's 2.71828, 1828459045235360287, and so on and so on. Now it's represented by exactly this infinite series, and we can think of this infinite series in terms of an algorithm. It's laying out a procedure for calculating more and more of these infinite decimals. So the advantage is we don't need to think about the object itself as being this infinite decimal going on forever and ever and ever and ever. We don't have to become philosophical about what does "forever and ever and ever" mean because we can just work with this formula. This formula is essentially encoding this decimal. The formula is a very finite thing; it presents us with a very simple algorithm that generates the decimal. So it's the same kind of idea as when we use something like this with a repeating decimal. We had some finite expression that actually encodes something that we can think of as going on forever, but the actual expression that we're using is finite. The expression is finite; it's a finite algorithm. So this is one big advantage: that the infinite series can be summed this way. Of course, there's a little bit of a catch. The series has to have the property that the terms that we're adding get small sufficiently fast so that there's some kind of convergence so that we do actually end up stabilizing and that these things don't start wandering around as we add more terms. So not all infinite series are summable in this way, but the convergent ones are, and we do need this theory of real numbers, it seems, to sum them. And this is one big advantage in thinking about real numbers this way—in this algorithmic way—that it allows us to sum convergent infinite series. Another big advantage of the algorithmic approach is that it allows us to define many functions. For example, the exponential function, a key function in modern analysis, which we can define this way along with Euler: the limit as n approaches infinity of (1 + x/n) to the nth power. So we're inputting x, and we're outputting this thing. Well, this thing is really an algorithm, or it's a procedure that allows us to calculate more and more digits of some decimal number. For example, suppose we want to calculate e squared, or the x of 2. So the x has the value 2; we're going to replace this with 2. So what we have to do is we have to calculate this expression for a given value of n, n equals 1, and then n equals 2, and then n equals 3, and so on, and keep going, and hopefully, the digits that we get will stabilize and give us ultimately a decimal expression. So what happens in this case? Well, in the case n equals 1, we have 1 plus 2 over 1 to the 1st power, which equals 3. When n is 2, we get 1 plus 2 over 2 squared, which is 4. When n is 3, 1 plus 2 over 3 cubed, which is this 4.629 repeated. When n is 4, we get 5.0625—it's a finite decimal or a decimal number. When n is 5, we get 5.3782 for another decimal number. And here, 1 plus 2 over 6 to the 6th power—well, it's some repeating decimal because it's a fraction, but actually, the repeating part is too long to easily contain here. So what's happening to these numbers? Are they progressing towards some goal? Are they stabilizing? Well, in fact, they are not stabilizing very clearly at this level here, and that's because we haven't chosen enough n's. We haven't gone far enough to even start seeing a pattern. It turns out that the limit of these things is this real number, 7.389056098, and so on. And to get the first three digits here, the 7.38, so in order to get 7.38 consistently, you need to go to n being around 1600. You need to go a long way in this series before you even start getting the first few terms. Alright, well, this can happen. There's no indication about how long we have to go before we get to what we want, but nevertheless, this thing does converge. And as we go further and further with increasing values of n, then we end up generating more and more and more of these digits, and this is e squared, which is exactly the square of that number e that we had on our previous slide. So this approach to real numbers as algorithms allows us to define many important functions of modern analysis, so it's a very key application of the idea. A fourth major advantage in this algorithmic approach is that it allows us to define and evaluate integrals. Integrals are a key tool for modern analysis and modern mathematics more generally. So, for example, and I'm just giving an overview here, this is not a careful exposition of either integrals or analytic functions or infinite series. I'm just giving you a taste of some of these ideas. So here's the function f of x equals e to the negative x squared. So it's that exponential function we were considering before with now a minus x squared input into it. If we graph it, we get something that looks like this. It's a famous bell curve shape. And let's say that we're interested in the integral from 0 to 1 of this function. And that means we're trying to estimate or find the area under this graph from 0 to 1. Okay, so there are various ways of trying to define what this integral is. And one way, which was introduced by the famous German mathematician Riemann in the middle of the 19th century, is in terms of a limit of approximating sums of rectangles. So what we can think of here is, say, we can divide the interval from 0 to 1 into n equal little pieces. And for each one of these little pieces, we're going to build a rectangle whose height is the function's value at, say, the rightmost endpoint of the interval. So in this case, we get all these little rectangles, which are just lying underneath the curve, and we can calculate the sum of all these little areas, and that's what this is. So here is the common width of the rectangle, and here are the various heights of the rectangles, summed from 1 to n, the values of the function. These are the values at these various points of this particular function. Alright, this kind of expression cannot be calculated within the framework of rational numbers, except in an approximate way. But the real numbers ostensibly give us a way to actually complete the calculation—to get something that's not approximate but actually something that's exactly correct. Okay, so what ends up happening is that we have to calculate this sum for various values of n. So, for example, when n is 2, then we have 1/2 of e to the negative 1/4 plus e to the negative 1, which turns out to be this. When n is 5, meaning we have 5 little rectangles sitting under the curve from 0 to 1, then the sum of the rectangles is 1/5 e to the negative 1/20 plus e to the negative 4/25 to e to the negative 1. These are the various heights of the rectangles. Work that out, and we get something like this. When n equals 10, 1/10, the same kind of sum with 10 terms, we get something like this. We need to make some remarks about the calculations here. So the calculation here is involving already these terms here, which themselves are given by algorithms. So we have an algorithm for e to the x, so we have an algorithm for e to the negative 1/4, and that's the kind of thing that we're using. We have two of those; we have to be able to sum them up and divide by two to get this term here. So this is not a case of having rational numbers that we're using; we're already using real numbers at each stage here. And then the idea is to look at these numbers, and as n gets big, to see if they start trending towards some values, and it turns out that they do. You want to go further than n equals 10, so as we keep going, we slowly start converging to a decimal that looks like this. Okay, so this is just a very quick introduction to integration, which we haven't talked at all about in this course up till now. We will eventually talk about integrals, but just to give you a sense of the claim that real numbers as algorithms do allow us to calculate integrals under functions, and this is a primary advantage and good reason for having real numbers as algorithms around. So this is something that a mathematician values highly. We like to be able to integrate; we like to be able to get answers to things like this, and real numbers are seemingly providing that framework for doing that. Real numbers ultimately, in terms of an algorithm, because contained in the idea of an integral is really the algorithm described by Riemann, but actually going back to Newton. So there are quite a few more advantages to real numbers in terms of algorithms, but they sort of follow the same kind of line, and they're often consequences of the ones I've already mentioned. In addition, we have the advantages that I laid out for infinite decimals in terms of infinite choice in my previous video. We have things like the existence of the intermediate value theorem, which is also really an algorithmic procedure. We have the existence of the fundamental theorem of algebra, the factorization of polynomials, which is also an algorithmic procedure. So there's a whole host of mathematical theorems that rely on real numbers, properties of real numbers, which are intimately connected with the algorithmic aspect in this respect that we're talking about now. So we have a lot of good reasons for wanting to have real numbers and wanting to have real numbers in terms of algorithms. However, the painful reality is that there are serious problems, so let's have a look at some of the difficulties. Alright, the first is that this theory is very, very difficult to set up. So what are some of the difficulties? Well, first of all, we have to decide what exactly is an algorithm? What are we going to be talking about? What is an algorithm? What is a computer program? What is a procedure? What kind of functions are we going to be considering when we talk about algorithms or computer programs? We're often thinking about having a certain machine, we're working in C++ or in Pascal or in Fortran or something. So what machine, what language, what formulas are you going to allow? There are some subtle issues but important issues about whether you're going to allow bounded or unbounded running times. So if you have an algorithm, say, that generates the nth digit of pi, and I'm interested in calculating the billionth digit of pi using your algorithm, a natural question is how long is this going to take me? Okay, so the issue here is, do I know ahead of time roughly how long this is going to take me to calculate this billionth digit? For example, you might tell me this is going to take me, you know, 24 hours, or maybe 24 days, or maybe 24 years, but if it's going to take me 24 centuries, then I probably want to know that before I start with the algorithm. And if you don't know how long it's going to take, it might be that your algorithm is such that it's unclear how long it's going to take. It might take 24 billion years—well, then that's a serious obstacle. So the question is, are we going to specify that algorithms are bounded or unbounded in running time? A related issue is, do we require certificates of boundedness? So if you come to me with an algorithm, and you claim it's a bounded algorithm, I might want to know, well, are you going to give me a proof of that fact, or am I just going to take your word for it? So is the proof of the boundedness of an algorithm part of the data that we're going to demand from our algorithms? So let's suppose that we've overcome these difficulties. Let's suppose that we've established what framework we're working in. We know exactly what an algorithm is in this context. We've decided what kind of machines we're working on, and so on, and we've answered these questions. Then there are these very, very significant challenges that loom ahead. We're going to have to define, of course, the operations—the operations of addition and multiplication, and then ultimately of subtraction and division too—but let's focus on these. So if you have one algorithm for a real number, and I have another algorithm for a real number—so algorithm 1 and algorithm 2—how are we going to combine those two algorithms to give a third algorithm for the sum of our numbers? If my algorithm generates a decimal number, and yours does too, if we add those decimals, we're going to get another decimal number, and that's going to be also given by an algorithm. The question is, how do you get algorithm 3 from algorithm 1 and algorithm 2? How do you define this operation at the level of algorithms? And you're going to have to worry that this is well-defined because there are lots of real numbers that have a whole bunch of different algorithms that define them, so you're going to have to have a check to make sure that your definition of addition of algorithms, whatever it may be, is actually well-defined. And you're going to, of course, have to do the same thing with multiplication, which is going to be even harder. And then, once you've done that, you're going to have to prove that the laws of arithmetic work. You're going to have to prove that the distributive law works for your definitions of addition and multiplication of algorithms. Now, let me point out to you a very serious challenge that you're going to have to deal with. So this is the same problem that we saw with the infinite choice approach to infinite decimals. The problem is, what happens when, at the decimal digit level, you end up with some nines? So let's say we're adding these two numbers, and here are the actual decimal numbers. We don't know what the algorithms are, just at the decimal number level. So one, eight, eight, and here is one plus four is five, but the next sum is nine. So we have to check what's happening before we can write down a five because there might be a six here if that nine spills over and gives us a carry. And here we have zero plus nine, that's nine; two plus seven, another nine; another nine, another nine, another nine, another nine, another nine. So the question is, what is your algorithm going to do at this exact point right here? Your algorithm for adding these decimals has got to come up with that digit. So your algorithm can detect that there's a nine summed next, and then another nine appearing, another nine here. And, for example, it can try to check the next thousand or million places, and if you don't get some nines at some point, well then you're alright, but if you get a total of nine for the next million places, at some point your algorithm is going to have to say, "Alright, wait a minute. We're going to have to stop checking and see whether we can otherwise determine what this thing is." So you have a big advantage working with algorithms rather than with infinite choice because you know what the algorithm is that generates this digit sequence, and you know what algorithm it is that generates this digit sequence, so in principle, it might be possible for you to analyze those algorithms and see whether this pattern of matching up numbers, adding up to nine, continues indefinitely or not. That's the question: Does the sequence of nines we're getting continue indefinitely, or does it eventually stop and give us something else? Because you have the algorithms, you are at least theoretically in a position to check that by analyzing the structure of the algorithms. But that's going to be a horrendously difficult thing to do, okay? And probably, to be honest, it's probably not even possible, but let's give you the benefit of the doubt, alright? It's going to be very, very, horrendously difficult, and you have to be able to deal with this. So you have to build into your algorithms for addition and multiplication this exact issue here of how to deal with nines that appear, that seem to go on forever, but you're not sure. Alright, in the year 2012, I'm confident in saying that no such theory exists. We're not even close to having a theory of real numbers as infinite decimals generated by algorithms in this fashion. It just does not exist. No one has come up with such a thing. It's just the reality of it. Now, I think this is a great project. It's a great project in the sense that even if we were able to make some small progress, it would be very interesting. So, for example, you might be able to generate a theory like this that works not with general algorithms but rather algorithms of a rather specialized kind—something like repeating decimals, but maybe just a little bit more complicated than repeating decimals. Again, I don't know of any theory that goes beyond repeating decimals, except when you're kind of looking at finite field extensions, which you can encode algorithmically if you want to, but something that's sort of significantly beyond finite field extensions—I don't know of such a theory. But it would be very interesting if someone were able to come up with such a thing. So I don't say it's impossible; maybe some clever people in the future will be able to derive and create such a theory, but currently, we do not have such a thing. So my criticism of this theory is, in some sense, I'm criticizing something that doesn't really exist. This is a dream; it's a dream currently. So even though it'd be great to have such a theory, we do not have such a theory. A second key problem is the non-uniqueness of algorithms. The exponential function, for example, that we talked about earlier, I gave you Euler's definition of it. Here is a quite different definition of that exponential function as an infinite series. In fact, you can sort of see when x is equal to 1, we're getting that value for e that we talked about earlier. Alright, so you could encode this infinite series in terms of an algorithm. Then we have two different algorithms that look, on the face of it, very different, which in fact are generating the same function. Unfortunately, there is no computable way of checking if two algorithms generate the same infinite decimal. There's no way of taking two random algorithms and methodically checking some condition to say, "Ah, these algorithms generate the same infinite decimal." There's no way of doing that. It's a very serious problem. So to give you an illustration of that, here is a specific example that rests on the classical number theoretical problem of whether there are any odd perfect numbers. So a perfect number is a number like 6, whose sum of its divisors, less than itself, equals the number itself. So 6 is the sum of its divisors, less than it is 1 plus 2 plus 3. Those are the divisors of 6, and they sum up to 6, so 6 is perfect. 28 is also perfect because its divisors are 1, 2, 4, 7, and 14, and they also sum up to twenty-eight. The question is, are there any odd perfect numbers? No one knows the answer to that famous number theoretical problem. So, unfortunately or fortunately, we could build in such a number theoretical conundrum into an algorithm. So here's a little algorithm to compute the nth digit of this decimal. Let's call it x. Here's what you do: First of all, you find the nth number. Let's call it k. So, for example, if n happens to be 8, the 8th odd number is 15, okay? Next, you calculate the sum s of all the divisors d, which are less than k, okay? So, for example, for 15, the divisors are 1, 3, and 5. We calculate the sum, that's 9, okay? And we're calling that s, okay? If s equals k, then we set the 8th digit to be equal to 1, and if s is not equal to k, then we set the nth digit to be 0. So here, is s equal to k? Is 9 equal to 15? No, it's not, which is telling us that 15 is not an odd perfect number. So the instruction is to set the 8th digit to be equal to zero, alright? So this is a little algorithm which is exactly going to output 0 here unless the nth odd number is perfect, in which case we'll get a 1. So we know that the first, you know, billion trillion numbers, whatever, there are no odd numbers that are perfect in there, so we can be sure that this number that we're generating with this algorithm starts 0.00000000 and goes on with zeros for at least a few billion, but we don't know whether this number is actually 0 or not. It's given by an algorithm. The algorithm is completely precise; we can code our computer, we can turn it on, but we don't know whether that number is zero or not. It might be that somewhere way down there, trillions and trillions and trillions and trillions further along, a one appears, okay? So we have no way at the level of algorithms to check whether this algorithm is the same as the zero algorithm that just says print zero, no matter what. So this is a very key problem that means that you can't have a surefire way of checking whether two algorithms generate the same decimal number. So these next two difficulties are related. The first is that it is difficult to find a canonical algorithm for an infinite decimal. When it comes to rational numbers like this one, we can reduce the denominator and the numerator by any common factors so that we get to a situation where the numerator and denominator do not have any common factors. And if we also agree that the denominator is always positive, then we get a canonical or standard form for a rational number. That means whenever we have an arithmetic problem involving a rational number, we can ask for the answer in standard form, which makes it very easy to check whether it's the right answer or not. With real numbers, we have no such situation. There are many different algorithms for a given infinite decimal, and there's no way of saying which one of these algorithms is superior to the other ones. There's no way of singling out a special algorithm that we're going to be using exclusively for that infinite decimal. So it becomes very hard to tell when two infinite decimals are equal, and so when I say "very hard," well, it's pretty well impossible. So it's impossible to tell when two infinite decimals are equal. Here's an example: This integral here with this expression involving 22/7 in this integral, that's a real number in the sense of this integral being interpreted in some fashion or another as an algorithm. In fact, there are a whole bunch of algorithms that one can use to sort of unravel what this integral is, but you take one of those, and you generate a real number. Here's another algorithm in terms of an infinite series. The infinite series has some binomial coefficient there, 2n choose n cubed, and then a fraction 42n plus 5 divided by 2 to the 12n plus 4—very curious expression. Then we take the whole thing and take its inverse. This is a formula of the great Indian mathematician Ramanujan. This is a formula that appeared in the University of Sydney exam in 1960. It turns out that they both evaluate to pi. They are both expressions for pi. There are among hundreds of expressions that are known, but let's say you were just given these two algorithms and asked, "Well, are the decimals the same?" It would be very, very hard to check that. Some very deep mathematical knowledge is necessary to recognize that both of these happen to be expressions for pi. So one of the consequences of this is that arithmetic with real numbers becomes vague and often almost tautological. Students don't practice doing arithmetic with real numbers because it's almost a non-existent subject except to the extent that it contains rational numbers inside it. But, for example, I've given you this problem in an earlier video to calculate square root 2 plus pi plus e in the real number framework. So the modern mathematician really has no answer to that other than to say, "Well, the answer is square root 2 plus pi plus e." That's the simplest expression that we have for this sum. Similarly, suppose you just sort of write down a random infinite series, summation n equals zero to infinity e to the minus (n + 5) times n cubed over n squared plus 1 factorial. Now, it might be that this thing, you know, evaluates to some rational number like 3/4—highly unlikely, but it's possible. But in the absence of knowing some particular special fact like that, we don't know how to sum such series in general. And if a mathematician was asked to evaluate that, about the only thing they could write down is this: "I’m going to solve this problem for you. Here's how you do it: There, I've just solved it. I've just evaluated that summation for you. By the way, if anybody knows a better answer, please let me know." Now, I'm going to evaluate this function. So here's a numerical expression: I'm going to evaluate that here, and this is the best I can do: Again, if you can do better than that, please let us know. And now I'm going to evaluate this—this is an integral. Alright, how do we evaluate that? Of course, I could write down an infinite decimal approximation, okay, but that's only a rational approximation. I can't actually write down that infinite decimal. If I want to say exactly what this is, really the only thing I can do is I can repeat the question back to you as an answer. And finally, in the real number framework, let's solve this problem: 1/2 plus 1/3 plus 1/5. Alright, if you're working in the rational numbers, well then there's only one possible answer, but if you're working in the real numbers, algorithms are fluid, and there's no special choice of algorithms. So, in keeping with the things above here, I think a student is justified in writing, "Well, there it is. There's the answer." Happy students doing arithmetic with real numbers! So I'm being a bit facetious here, but this is an issue. The issue is that we can't actually effectively do arithmetic with real numbers at all. And that's why first-year calculus books, after they introduce real numbers, rarely ever have a section on "Now let's practice a little bit of real number arithmetic to get you used to it, to get you understanding how it works." There's never a section like that because the arithmetic is vacuous in some sense. It's a necessary consequence of this imprecision that we get when we look at things with an algorithmic point of view. Now, there are many other difficulties that arise in mathematics as a consequence of this point of view towards real numbers, but I'm going to stop here. These are sort of elementary considerations that are very close to the core of the definition. So I think the overall argument that I'm making here is that trying to define real numbers in terms of algorithms doesn't work. It has not been done; there are too many technical obstacles, and people are just playing some fantasy game when they talk about this theory as if the theory already exists. The theory is not there, even though we would very much like it to be there because that's what we use to do our infinite series, to do our function evaluations, to do our integrals, and to do many other things. So we see a serious disconnect in modern mathematics between rigorous thinking and the actual practical application of what we're doing. Now, most pure mathematicians are going to be reluctant to face the music here. The idea is to avoid thinking about this problem because most mathematicians are aware at some level that it's there. The agreement has been to "let's not think about it too much," let's call all such discussion, "Oh, that's philosophy," or, "That has to do with the foundations of mathematics, which doesn't need to concern us." But it ought to concern us very much because it has very serious consequences for mathematics. And once you start adopting lazy attitudes towards rigor at this level, it starts infiltrating other areas in mathematics. Students learn that there are certain places where it's allowed to be woefully vague, where it's allowed to be sort of hand-waving. It's not an ideal situation, I think. Next time, what we're going to do is look at a very important object historically, and one that many of you will probably like very much: this magical object called pi. Is it really a number? That is not so clear. I'm going to introduce the idea that maybe pi is something slightly different from a number in the usual sense, so we'll have a historical look at formulas for pi. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.

so these two sequences converge in this sense but and here is the important point they converge in a stronger sense than just that they converge in this kosice sense which is that the quadrants is between SK and RL go to 0 as K and L increase in other words it's not just pairing sk with RK where the indices are the same we're comparing an arbitrary element in the s sequence with an arbitrary element in the our sequence and as long as the indices are sufficiently big then the two points are close so here for example if K and L are both big but not necessarily equal for example K could be a thousand and L might be 3000 then still if we look at s a thousand wherever it is and our sub 3000 those two points are going to be very close together the quadrants between them is going to be close to zero so this is the Koshi notion of convergence it doesn't apply over here these two sequences do not converge in the cosy sense because well if they keep on doing like this taking sort of equal steps like this then we cannot expect for example S sub thousand and R sub 3,000 to be close to each other they're not going to be close to each other yes s a thousand is going to be close to our a thousand but it's not going to be close to our thousand and one some dramatic difference between these two sequences and these two sequences and so the analyst says when we want to define real numbers as equal those classes of sequences we've got to be working with this Koshi notion of convergence so that's a very important idea in modern analysis in fact very important idea unfortunately the reality is that trying to set up a theory of real numbers as equivalence classes of Cauchy sequences of rational numbers is very very difficult and it's fraught with logical complications and in fact it's rarely attempted in its entirety so what happens is that students are told about this and they might be told a few things about it and maybe even a few things are proven but in terms of having a systematic complete layout of this theory it's almost never done it requires more than just a few days effort and when you look at it closely as we'll see well the homes are quite significant so something to keep in mind here with this discussion it's very easy for us to get confused and very easy for us to start crossing over from mathematics into philosophy I'm not really interested in in philosophy in terms of mathematics I would like to remove philosophy from mathematics and I think one way of orienting ourselves in this direction is to concentrate on explicit examples and concrete computations these are two things that can anchor us to reality so if we have some complicated theory and we're not quite sure do we understand this theory or not we look for explicit examples we try to make sure that the examples actually correspond to the definitions so we match up the examples with the definitions and we try to make concrete computations we say let's put this into practice and see what happens so in our next video we're going to move towards a more careful understanding of these various issues and a good place to start is by looking at the structure of the rational numbers the rational numbers are the framework in which we are working and trying to set up these real numbers the rationals are much more complicated than people think they have a very deep structure in fact and it's a very good idea to understand a little bit that deep structure of the rational numbers so we'd have an initial look at that next time I hope you'll join me for that I'm Norman Wildberger
