# 102 - Limits and rational poly on-sequences ｜ Real numbers + limits Math Foundations 102 ｜ N J Wildberger [pftEWRD4Tac].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today, we're going to be talking about an extension of polynomials on sequences where we move to rational polynomial sequences. This will also give us an opportunity to introduce, in a very gentle way, the idea of a limit rather informally and in a very simple example. We'll also be introducing some important new terminology and an extension of our number field to include infinity, which might be rather interesting. Before I get going with today's lecture, though, I would like to do something else, which I'm going to be doing a little bit more regularly as we go along. As you know, I'm critiquing modern mathematics here; I'm questioning the foundations of analysis. My claim is that the modern foundations of analysis don't really work, and so I think it's useful to occasionally present you with some evidence of that from the literature. Today, what I'm going to do is just very quickly have a look at a lovely book. Periodically, I'm going to show you some books, and we're going to talk about them, especially in relation to foundational aspects. I should say that all the books I'm going to show you are books that I value highly. I'm not going to bring any books and show you them unless I already respect and value them as important contributions. So, even though I may be saying some negative things about the books I'm showing you, I still think quite highly of them overall. The book I'm showing you today is this one here; it's called Selected Papers on Calculus. It's already about 50 years old, and it's by the Mathematical Association of America. It's a very interesting compilation of many articles that have appeared over the years in the American Mathematical Monthly, which is one of the most prestigious and widely read of all popular mathematics journals. All these articles have to do with calculus in one way or another. There are probably about 150 different articles, many very nice ones with a lot of interesting material. What I want to point out to you is that, scanning the articles here—which range from the history of calculus to pedagogy, talking about functions, continuity, differentiation, derivatives, polynomials, integration techniques, summation, sequences, and special numbers like pi and e—despite the many articles found in this book, very few of them actually deal with foundational questions. The pure foundational questions of how one sets up analysis, such as the critical issues of how we define what a function is, the definitions of the trigonometric functions, the exponential function, the log function, or the definitions of the various constants like pi and e, and more generally, how one sets up the real numbers to begin with—these all-important issues, these foundational problems, and others having to do with the nature of the derivative and the definition of the integral, are basically all finessed in this book, as they are in many other treatments. There is simply this blanket assumption that all those issues are more or less bedded down and not even worth raising and examining. So, it's like a survey of building or construction which doesn't have any chapters on how to lay the foundations of a house. There's something askew here, especially considering the very significant foundational problems that beset modern analysis in the early part of the last century. There was a lot of debate 100 years ago about how to set up analysis, about what continuity meant, what integration meant, and various other things—in particular, the real numbers. How does one set them up? These questions have now been largely relegated to ancient history, and there's a very convenient assumption by the academy to pretend that all such problems are nonexistent. There's a big elephant sitting in the corner of the room, and as long as we don't pay any attention to it and it doesn't pay any attention to us, everything will be fine. So, in subsequent videos, I'm going to show you some other textbooks, and we're going to have a closer look at how modern mathematics actually addresses these issues that I'm discussing in this series. Okay, so today we're going to talk about rational polynomial sequences, or maybe rational polyon sequences for short. I'm going to follow a, by now, hopefully familiar framework. We started off with this course talking about natural numbers, integers, fractions, and working our way to rational numbers. Natural numbers like this: integers were defined in terms of pairs of natural numbers—so, 2 less 3, that was the way we introduced what we now call minus one. We talked about fractions like 1/7, also built from natural numbers, and then sort of putting these two things together—integers over integers making rational numbers—and they are the sort of prime objects for doing arithmetic in everyday life, the rational number field. Then, later, when we started introducing algebra, we went up a level of sophistication and started talking about polynomials, or as we preferred, poly numbers. Analogous to this, we had a sort of compatible structure of natural number poly numbers, integral poly numbers, fractional poly numbers, and rational poly numbers. Here's a typical natural polynomial, and then taking differences of them yields integral poly numbers. That's one way of introducing integral poly numbers. Another way is just to consider poly numbers with integer coefficients, which amounts to roughly the same thing. We can take ratios of them to create fractional poly numbers, and if we do that with integer poly numbers, then we get the field of rational poly numbers, which is second in importance in mathematics only to the field of rational numbers. So, rational numbers and rational poly numbers—these are fields that support our four fundamental operations: addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division, with just a little bit of a problem—we're not allowed to divide by zero. Otherwise, there's a kind of uniformity and a completeness in these kinds of arithmetic domains. Okay, so having understood this framework, we're going to now go up a level to considering sequences, or on sequences, built up from the basic, similar framework. We're mostly interested today in these rational poly numbers and extending them to on sequences. So, let's have a reminder of what these objects are like and how we do arithmetic with rational poly numbers. Now, in modern mathematics, these objects are often called rational functions, which is unfortunate terminology. The term "rational poly numbers" or "rational polynomials" is, I think, superior notation and more properly captures what we're doing. So, I remind you that a rational poly number is a formal ratio of two integral poly numbers, and there is an important notion of equality: that P/Q = R/S precisely when PS = RQ. These four are all integral poly numbers, so you can think of them as polynomials with coefficients which are integers—polynomials in alpha. For example, here is a rational polynomial, and we'll write the fraction not with a slant like this but rather with a horizontal bar because it's just easier to write. So, this one here, 1 - Alpha over 2 + Alpha squared, that's the same as Alpha - Alpha squared over 2 Alpha + Alpha cubed. And why is that? It's because if you multiply 1 - Alpha by this, it's the same as what you get when you multiply this by Alpha - Alpha squared. And we can see that in another way by just realizing that to go from here to here, we've multiplied the top and the bottom by the same poly number, namely Alpha. So, to go from here to here, we've multiplied by Alpha; to go from here to here, we've multiplied by Alpha. That has the effect of making sure that this kind of equality is maintained. So, these two rational poly numbers may look different, but they are actually officially exactly the same objects for us. It's going to be a basic but important point in today's lecture. Okay, and we can do arithmetic with these things. So, for example, if we want to add this rational poly number and this one, well, then what we do is we take this times this plus this times this, all over this times this, just exactly the same way we add fractions. Of course, there's simplification here necessary to get it in standard form, top and bottom. So, of course, arithmetic with fractions, with ordinary rational numbers, is a necessary preliminary to doing algebra with these rational poly numbers. We can multiply two of them, in which case we just multiply the numerators and multiply the denominators, and we can also divide one by the other, which amounts to multiplying this one by the reciprocal of this one—what you get when you interchange the numerator and denominator. So, I hope we're all comfortable with the arithmetic or the algebra of rational poly numbers. And let's just make sure that we're all on the same level here—that we all appreciate that these rational poly numbers are not functions. Okay, so they are not functions, first of all, because we have not got a comprehensive definition of what a function is—that's actually a very problematic issue. For example, when we write down something like this, T = 4 - Alpha + Alpha^5 + 7 Alpha squared, that is not a function. It is simply an expression that we write down in terms of Alphas. So, the expression itself lives above any particular interpretation of it. Someone can come along and say, "Oh, well, that looks like a function from the rational numbers to the rational numbers," and someone else can come along and say, "Oh no, that looks like a sequence—we input a natural number and get out a rational number." Someone else might say, "No, no, that's a function from the complex numbers to the complex numbers." There might be lots of other possible interpretations of this, but for us, the object itself is just the expression. We do not need to specify a particular interpretation of the expression. Okay, so earlier in this series, I gave an entire lecture on this issue of the difference between object-oriented mathematics and expression-oriented mathematics, and you will know that I encourage an expression-oriented approach to mathematical work. Okay, there's another important notion that we need to consider, and that's the evaluation of rational poly numbers. So, for example, if we stick with this one here—this T, it's a rational poly number—what is T of 1? Well, T of 1 is what you get when you replace the alpha with the number 1. So, 4 - 1 + 1/5 is 1 over 5 + 7 * 1 squared, for a total of 4 over 12, which is a rational number, also equal to 1/3. Another example: we could also plug in an integer, say minus 2, put Alpha equal to -2. We get 4 + 2 + (-2)^5, which is -32, over 5 + 7 * 4, 28, giving us -26 over 33. So, in general, the rule is that if we have a rational polynomial which is A over B, where A and B are integral poly numbers, and if, say, K is an integer, then P of K is, by definition, the rational number A of K over B of K. So, we have a well-defined notion of what it means to evaluate a poly number at an integer, and we're evaluating two poly numbers and taking the corresponding rational number we get. So, we can evaluate rational poly numbers at, say, integers. So, now that we're all happy with rational poly numbers, let's use them to extend our notion of on sequences to consider more general infinite sequences. We're replacing the term "infinite sequences" with "on sequences," short for "ongoing sequences." So, the idea is we're going to start with a rational poly number, and we're going to generate a sequence from it. We'll call that a rational polyon sequence, and here's our notation. It's already familiar by now, I hope: left square bracket, the actual rational poly number, and then a right-pointed bracket. An alternate notation is we're going to replace the alpha in P with the letter n. N is our favorite number for sequences, and that expression will also indicate to us that we have a sequence here. And the crucial thing about a sequence, or an on sequence, is that we can pick out the K element, where K is usually a natural number for us—so 1, 2, 3, 4, and so on. And the definition is that if we take this rational poly number P and then generate this on sequence, then the K element in that on sequence is, by definition, P of K. Now, that's in general a rational number, not an integer—so K is an integer, but what's on this side here will typically be a rational number. So, let us look at an example. Here is our rational poly number P: it is 1 + Alpha - Alpha squared over 2 + Alpha cubed. So, one integral polynomial in the numerator, another integral polynomial in the denominator. Then what is the corresponding on sequence? We can write it as the sequence P, or to be more explicit, we can write it this way, with this expression put in here, the alphas replaced with n's. So, the sequence 1 + n - n^2 over 2 + n^3. Okay, and what is this sequence? It's the sequence that you get by starting to calculate the values of this thing—first when n is equal to one, then when n is equal to two, then when n is equal to three, and so on. So, for example, when n is equal to one, we get 1 + 1 - 1^2—that's a total of one on the top—over 2 + 1^3, that's 3. If n is equal to 2, we would have 1 + 2 - 4, that's minus 1 altogether, over 2 + 2^3, which is 10. So that should be -1/10. And the next one, let's just check that: if n is equal to 3, 1 + 3 - 9, that's 4 - 9, that's -5, over 2 + 3^3, that's 2 + 27, that is in fact 29. Alright, so if our arithmetic is good, we can carry on generating more and more terms, and of course the thing doesn't necessarily stop—it sort of keeps on going. Depending on how much patience and computing power we have, we can keep on generating more and more terms in this ongoing sequence. So, now we've extended our notion of infinite sequences, or on sequences, in an important way. We've gone from constant on sequences to poly number on sequences, and now to rational poly number on sequences. So, these are now much more general kinds of on sequences, and they're very important for us in calculus, as we'll see. These are very much sort of workhorses of calculus. So, it all seems very good, but in fact there is a big logical problem. What I've told you in the last few slides is logically weak. There is a key problem that I have sloughed over and not mentioned, and I wonder how many of you have caught on to what that is. So, it's now a great exercise to go over these few pages and to look very carefully at what I've done and ask yourself, okay, what is the problem here? There's a logical problem. So, you might want to stop the video and go over these issues, have a look, spend perhaps some days thinking about it. Okay, there's a logical problem, and you want to find out what it is. It's not particularly subtle—it's pretty straightforward, and once you see it, it's kind of obvious, right? So, please have a look and have a think about what is the logical problem that I've been ignoring here. So, I hope you found what that logical problem is yourselves. Okay, and it comes down to a very common source of difficulty in mathematics—elementary but common—which is that division by zero is illegal. That's the one operation with rational numbers that we are not allowed to do. And unfortunately, we have built in the possibility of division by zero so far in our treatment of these rational poly number on sequences. For example, suppose that we had looked at the rational poly number Alpha over 2 - Alpha. This is a rational poly number because it's an integral poly number over another integral poly number. Okay, here's the associated rational polyon sequence: n over 2 - n. What's the sequence associated with it? Well, we just start generating values—when n is 1, we get 1 over 2 - 1, that's 1 over 1. When n is 2, we get 2 over 2 - 2, that unfortunately is 2 over 0. When n is 3, we get 3 over 2 - 3, that's 3 over -1. My arithmetic is not very good today. There we go. And we could keep on going, and there would be no more problems. However, there's no escaping the fact that when n is equal to 2, we have forced ourselves to make an illegal operation—2 over 0. All bets are off. This object collapses as a logically coherent thing. It just does not work. Let's look at another example, slightly more complicated. Here we have a rational poly number Q: 1 over 3 - 4 Alpha - 2 Alpha squared + Alpha cubed. Alright, so we can generate values. When you plug in Alpha = 1, then you get 3 - 4 - 2 + 1, that's 4 - 6, that'd be minus 2, so we get -1/2. When n is 2, hopefully it'll be minus 1/5. And what happens when n is equal to 3? Well, it's not so obvious here, but let's have a look: 3 - 12, and then this will be -18 + 27. So altogether, 3 + 27, that's 30, and -2 - 18, that's -30. We're going to get zero in the denominator again. This just does not work. This is not a legitimate object. So that's certainly a problem, but there's another kind of problem also—a more subtle kind of problem but just as important. Consider the following example, which seems to be quite okay: the example R = Alpha over 1 + Alpha. The associated on sequence n over 1 + n. This is clearly unsurprising. This same poly number R is also equal to this poly number—these are exactly the same poly numbers. The numerator here is obtained from this one by multiplying by 1 - Alpha. The denominator here is obtained from this one by multiplying by 1 - Alpha. Since we're multiplying by the same things top and bottom, there's no question that this object and this object are exactly the same. We've agreed that they're exactly the same poly numbers, exactly the same rational poly numbers. The associated on sequence we ought to write like this: n - n^2 over 1 - n^2. So, what is the associated sequence? Well, when we evaluate this thing when n is equal to 1, the rule is to plug n = 1 into the numerator. We get 0. Plug n into the denominator, we get 1 - 1, another 0. We're getting 0 over 0, which is also undefined. Subsequent values are okay—2/3, 3/4, and so on. So, these two sequences agree perfectly, except at the first term. There's a difference: this one was well-defined, this one was not well-defined, yet we said that these were actually going to be the same rational poly numbers. So, we have a problem here: the evaluation of a rational poly number is not well defined. We were too hasty, a little bit too simple-minded, in assigning a meaning to the evaluation of a rational poly number. So, we have to go back and rethink that important definition. Let's have a closer look at one of the offending examples. Here, these two expressions as rational poly numbers are equal. This is the example we had on the last sheet—this one just obtained from this one by multiplying the top and bottom by 1 - Alpha. Now, these two expressions agree at all rational values except at 1. So, when you take any rational value and plug it into both of these things, you're going to get the same expression unless R is equal to 1. When R is equal to 1, this will evaluate to 1/2, but this will evaluate to 0/0. The reason is that the polynomial that we've multiplied this one by to get this, which is 1 - Alpha over 1 - Alpha, evaluates to 1 for every rational value except r = 1. In other words, this has the value 1 if you replace Alpha with any number whatsoever—so obviously except when you replace Alpha with 1, in which case you get 0/0, which is undefined. So, I think we can see the reason why things are going wrong. These two expressions are agreeing completely, except when R just has this particular value of 1 because of the sort of artificial 0/0 that multiplying by this term introduced. In particular, if R is a rational number which is close to 1, close to 1 but not actually equal to 1, like perhaps 0.998 or 1.3, then we know that replacing this with r gives the same value—these things are actually equal. So, the two expressions agree even for R very close to 1; it's just that exactly at 1 they happen to disagree because of the 0/0 that's being introduced there. Alright, so let's go back to this issue of evaluating a rational poly number, and let's be a little bit more careful about this definition. We'll suppose that P equals A/B, where A and B are integral poly numbers, and we'll suppose that K is an integer—or actually, for sequences, just a natural number will do. The idea is we want to evaluate P at this natural number K. Okay, we're going to do two things: we're going to look at the numerator A, and we're going to write it by factoring out Alpha - K. Perhaps Alpha - K is a factor of this polynomial—this is an integral polynomial, so maybe it has Alpha - K as a factor, maybe it has Alpha - K squared as a factor, or Alpha - K cubed. We're going to factor out as many of these Alpha - K's as we can and write A as a product of some other sort of quotient polynomial, Alpha tilde, say, times this term Alpha - K to the power of n. The crucial thing about this is that it does not have Alpha - K as a factor. So, by Descartes' factor theorem, if it doesn't have Alpha - K as a factor, it means that when we evaluate it at K, we're not getting zero. Now, it might be that this n is actually zero, so it might be that A doesn't have any factors of Alpha - K. That's okay, so we include that possibility, where n is zero. We do the same thing for B: we write it as some polynomial B hat times some power Alpha - K to the power of m, say, where again, this polynomial B hat has the property that when you evaluate it at K, you don't get zero. So here, n and m are natural numbers augmented with zero, so we're including all natural numbers—1, 2, 3, and also the possibility that the exponents are zero. So, this is a preliminary to defining what P of K is. Having done this, we are now in a position to define P of K, and here's the definition. It might look a little bit surprising; there's some new things involved here. Alright, first of all, there are three different cases depending on the relationship between these numbers n and m: If n is bigger than m—in other words, if there are more factors of Alpha - K in the numerator than there are in the denominator—then we define P of K to be zero. If they're exactly equal, if there's the same number of Alpha - K's in the numerator and in the denominator, then we define P of K to be Alpha hat of K over B hat of K. In other words, we just look at this thing over this thing—essentially, we're canceling out the Alpha - K to the power of n and the Alpha - K to the power of m, top and bottom, because the exponents are the same in this case. The crucial thing is these are nonzero numbers, because by assumption, this is nonzero, and this is also nonzero, so this rational number actually does make sense. But there's a third case, the case when n is less than m—in other words, where there are more factors of Alpha - K in the denominator than in the numerator. In that case, we are going to define P of K to be infinity. Wait a minute, what does that mean? Well, this sideways eight is a new symbol that we're introducing. It's just a new symbol. Its name is rather grandiosely "infinity"—that's what we're going to call this sideways eight. You can just think of it as something that we draw on the paper; it looks pretty, it's like a lemniscate. And we're going to agree that if this exponent is less than this exponent, then P of K is infinity. Admittedly, this is not a rational number—right? It's definitely not a rational number; it's a new expression that is outside our current number system. And it's just one of the realities of mathematics that at some point, it's necessary to think about extending our number system to include a number that plays the role—or an object that plays the role—of infinity. That's what we're doing now. So, we're going to talk more about this—this is a very interesting development. But basically, we have no choice; we have to do this if we want to have a sort of consistent understanding of the sequences generated by rational poly numbers. Okay, so this is a very important definition. It probably requires a few hours of thinking about—right? Write out this definition, stare at it. Now, let's have a look at some examples—always the best way to understand and to start to appreciate what the definition actually means. Okay, without examples, this is all sterile. Alright, so let's look at an example. Here is the polynomial P = Alpha - Alpha squared over 1 - Alpha squared. Let's think about evaluating it at the number one. It's obviously going to be problematic because in the numerator, we have 1 - 1, which is zero, and in the denominator, we also have 1 - 1, which is zero. So, what we have to do is factor the numerator and the denominator, and we're particularly interested in factors of the form Alpha - 1. Okay, in this case, it's a little bit easier if we use 1 - Alpha, because the arithmetic is just a little bit easier. So, I'm going to factor a 1 - Alpha out there and a 1 - Alpha out there. 1 - Alpha over 1 - Alpha, it's the same as Alpha - 1 over Alpha - 1. Alright, so having done that, we see that this thing really is the same as Alpha over 1 + Alpha. So, 1 - Alpha appears with an exponent of one in the numerator and also one in the denominator. That's that middle case. The middle case means that we basically factor these things out, we disregard them, and just consider the rest, which is Alpha over 1 + Alpha, and plug 1 into that. So, this is the A hat, and this is the B hat in the former notation. So, we just plug Alpha = 1 here and here to get 1 over 2. And okay, we can think about also roughly a kind of a limit that, if we look at this expression here or we look at this expression here, if R is a number, a rational number, that's approaching 1, then the value of this thing is going to be the same thing as the value of this thing, as long as R is not equal to 1. And then, as the value approaches 1, well, this is going to approach 1/2. So, another way of thinking about what we're doing here is we're really sort of thinking about the limit as R approaches 1 of P of R. But we haven't defined limits yet, so this is just a rather informal observation that what we're doing here is a kind of an algebraic equivalent of taking a limit, for those of you who have had some calculus before. Alright, so now we're in a position to write down the on sequence for this rational polyon sequence. The value at 1 is not 0/0—the value at 1 is 1/2, as we've just computed. The value at 2 is 2/3, then at 3, 3/4, then 4/5, and so on. So, it gives us a completely legitimate on sequence, even though at first glance you might say this is undefined when n is equal to 1. So, those of you who are used to calculus and treating functions in calculus, there's some adjustment to be made here. We are not thinking about this thing as primarily a function—that is not what it is. It's a rational poly number, and as a rational poly number, this thing and this thing are exactly equal. In calculus, if you were thinking of these as functions in calculus, you would have to say that as functions, these were almost equal, but not quite equal, because their values at 1 were not the same. We are not adopting that point of view. These are exactly the same objects for us, and so the on sequences that they generate have to be exactly the same. And so, we are forced to introduce our evaluation as we've done to talk consistently about the on sequence associated with a rational poly number. Let's look at another example, a bit more complicated. Here we have P = 6 - 5 Alpha + Alpha squared over -6 + 20 Alpha - 8 Alpha squared + Alpha cubed. So, if you set Alpha equal to 1 in here, it's not too hard to see you're going to get 2 over -3, or -2/3. If you plug 2 in, well, you're going to get 6 - 10 + 4, that's zero on the top, and in the bottom here, you're going to get -6 + 20 - 32 + 8, which is also zero. And well, these other values are made a little bit simpler by realizing that the polynomials that are involved here can be factored. So, the numerator can be factored as Alpha - 3 times Alpha - 2, and this denominator can be factored as Alpha - 4 times Alpha - 2 squared. So, that makes it clear what's going to happen when we plug in Alpha = 2: we're going to get 0/0 because of these terms here, at least when we have the naive approach. But now, with our more sophisticated notion of evaluation, we know that that's not quite what we're going to get. To evaluate this thing at 2, what we need to do is we need to look at these factors of Alpha - 2 that are appearing in the numerator and denominator, and notice that the exponent in the denominator is bigger than the exponent in the numerator—there's more Alpha - 2's in the denominator than there is in the numerator. That means that our definition is that P of 2 is our new symbol, infinity. How about evaluating it at 3? Well, this form of it makes it kind of obvious that the numerator is going to be zero, and the denominator is going to be nonzero, so we're going to get zero as the quotient. And when Alpha is equal to 4, or when n is equal to 4, we get a zero in the denominator here and no Alpha - 4 term in the numerator. So again, that's the third case—we must put in infinity. Then at 5, we're out of the woods—then we have 5 - 3, which is 2 times 5 - 2, which is 3, over 5 - 4, which is 1, times 5 - 2, that's 3 squared. So that'll be 6/9, which is 2/3. So, the associated on sequence to this poly number is -2/3, and then infinity, and then zero, and then another infinity, and then 2/3, and then, well, we could evaluate some more just by substituting in here and evaluating. So, we necessarily get on sequences whose entries are not just rational numbers, but also involve this new object—maybe we shouldn't call it a number yet; it's a new expression, this sideways 8, which we're calling infinity, rather grandiosely. Probably it would be better if we called it something else, but there's no way to change the established convention with regard to this symbol. We have to call it infinity, and it's actually not such a bad idea anyway, as we'll now see by actually looking at a picture of these rational poly numbers. So, we've spent a little bit of time graphing poly numbers or polynomials. The idea with rational poly numbers is much the same, except that it's a little bit more complicated, and we have to be prepared to use rational numbers. Here's our first example of the rational polynomial Alpha - Alpha squared over 1 - Alpha squared, and I'm writing it here sort of more in calculus style with an x and a y, reflecting our x and y graphs that we like to draw. So, how do we come up with a plot like this? Well, it's exactly the same kind of thing that you do before: you plug in various values of x, say x = 2, that would give us 2 - 4, that'd be minus 2, over 1 - 4, that'd be minus 3. So, -2 over -3, that'd be 2/3. So, I have to go up to the 2/3 level, and I would mark a point there. So, if you calculate lots of points and then you just connect them with little lines, then you get this graph that looks partially like this. Of course, this is just part of the picture; there's more outside, and of course, as we become more and more refined, this is just my very rough drawing of this thing, not very accurate. The associated on sequence is just obtained when we restrict ourselves to the values 1, 2, 3, and 4, and so on. So, the value at 1 is a half, the value at 2 is 2/3, the value at 3 is 3/4, and so on. There's a few things to notice about this graph, of course. There's something special happening at x = -1 and at x = 1, because the denominator is zero at both of those. The value at x = 1 turns out to be sort of an optical illusion because of the common factor of 1 - x in the numerator and denominator. But at x = -1, we have a more crucial kind of singularity here. The graph really does kind of go further and further down as we approach minus one from the right, and here, as we approach minus one from the left, it goes up and up and up. So, it would be quite correct to say that the value of this thing at minus one is infinity. What about this one here? That was our second example: x - 3 times x - 2 over x - 4 times x - 2 squared. Well, that's a little bit more complicated. There's now a singularity at 2 and 4, corresponding to the two factors x - 4 and x - 2 in the denominator. The graph looks something like this, and this goes off to zero. So, we're going to talk more about graphs of rational poly numbers like this. I don't expect you to be able to graph these so easily on your own—we'll talk a little bit more about these. These are going to play a very important role in calculus; these are very, very important objects. This is just an introduction to them. And here's the sequence that we get by looking at the values at 1, at 2, at 3, at 4, and 5, and so on. At 1, we decided we had -2/3. At 2, we had infinity—that was our sideways eight. At 3, we have zero—you can see it going through zero there. At 4, you can see again the infinite aspect of it. At 5, it becomes 2/3, and then it decreases from that on toward zero. So, these infinities that are appearing here, of course, are very interesting, and they're reflecting the fact that the graphs of these functions are doing something interesting at these values—at 2 and 4 here, and at minus 1 here. The limit of the values really is—well, it's going sort of down, down, down, sort of towards minus infinity, you might say. And when we approach 2 from this side, it's going up, up, up towards plus infinity. Maybe even in some sense, the values kind of agree that maybe the infinite in this direction and the infinite in that direction somehow meet—that's what the notation is suggesting. Okay, so we've opened up a new can of worms here. We've opened up the possibility of extending our number system to include infinity. And you may know that I don't like infinite sets, okay, and I'm very modest about things like this. So, we are not proposing that we are now at the level of gods, okay, that we are now somehow superior to what we were yesterday because we've introduced this symbol. No, it's just a symbol—it's an expression that we write down on the paper, which has a very specific and well-defined and coherent mathematical meaning. Next time, we're going to talk a little bit more about the possible arithmetic that we might get by extending our favorite rational number system to include this new expression, infinity. Is it possible? Does it work? It's a favorite subject of amateur mathematicians, so we're going to go down a little bit of that track next time. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger—thanks for listening. [Music]

# 106 - What exactly is a limit？？ ｜ Real numbers and limits Math Foundations 106 ｜ N J Wildberger [K4eAyn-oK4M].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day I'm Norman Wildberger today we're going to introduce a very important notion in the modern analysis the idea of a limit and not surprisingly we have a somewhat different take on limit than in most treatments I think that even if you're professional mathematician there may be something in this video that's new for you so let's start out by just an intuitive understanding here's a sequence 1 1/2 1/4 1/8 1/16 we intuitively feel that the numbers are getting smaller and they are approaching in some sense zero so we might say that the limit of the sequence looks like it's tending towards zero here's another sequence of terms getting smaller 1 1/2 1/3 1/4 1/5 approaching again 0 at least it looks like it this is a different kind of situation where we have numbers 0 and then 1/2 then 3/4 7/8 fifteen sixteenths now it appears as if the numbers are approaching one so in each case it appears that the sequence of numbers on the Left approaches the number on the right if you keep going of course there's some difficulties with this because the left-hand side the sequences are not really well defined just specifying a few terms doesn't of course layout the full sequence and more importantly the notion of a sequence itself is highly problematic we have not defined the notion of a general sequence like we've been rather careful to restrict ourselves to rather specific so-called on sequences associated with polynomials or poly numbers and rational functions or rational poly numbers in any case the idea of becoming clear about what approaching a limit means is a 19th century development and I guess most of the credit goes to great French mathematician Koshi who I guess asked the question what does it exactly mean for a sequence to approach a limit and the answer that he gave that was further developed by others became a cornerstone for modern analysis the notion of a limit Koshi and then later Karl buyer Strasse formalized the so called epsilon-delta definition of a limit at least according to the usual story and this is a rather technical formulation of what it means for a sequence to approach a limit for example the limit of the sequence s of n as n approaches infinity equals a or alternatively a slightly different context the limit of a function f of X as X approaches a equals L so in both of these cases the idea of what these things mean was encoded in a certain logical structure involving two arbitrary generally small numbers epsilon and Delta so generally refer to as the epsilon delta approach to limits unfortunately this theory does not really work okay there's serious logical gaps not surprisingly with the current modern treatment the epsilon delta treatment of limits even though ironically this is perceived by the Academy to be some kind of high point of rigor and it's perceived to be a particularly insightful and carefully laid-out definition but in fact when you look more carefully you see that it is not exactly so so most calculus texts are wrong when it comes to the notion of limit in fact the the notion of limits that's usually given is not only problematic logically but it's # 118 - Real fish, real numbers, real jobs ｜ Real numbers and limits Math Foundations 118 ｜ N J Wildberger [VUdFdlQNfpg].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, everyone. I'm Norman Wildberger. In today's video, I want to do something a little bit different. I want to go back to a paper that I wrote fifteen years ago called Real Fish, Real Numbers, Real Jobs, where I tried to start convincing my fellow mathematicians that there was something unsatisfactory about our approach to real numbers. This article appeared in The Mathematical Intelligencer, which is a very prominent expository journal that covers a wide range of mathematics for a very broad audience. The actual issue was Volume 21, Number 2, in 1999. The paper is not that long, and I'm going to inflict it upon you—I'm going to read it to you today. I hope you'll enjoy it because it's written in a somewhat tongue-in-cheek fashion, a little bit more lighthearted perhaps than my current approach to real numbers, which is a little bit more forceful and take-no-prisoners. And maybe we'll talk about that after I read the paper to you. Real Fish, Real Numbers, Real Jobs There are good times ahead for biologists. The electronics revolution will plateau in the early decades of the new millennium, and quantum computing, gene manipulation, and pet cloning will kick in. We'll see bumper crops of biology graduates pouring into expanded university departments, research institutes, and biotech companies. Some decades later, however, with hemoglobin-monitoring wristwatches and desktop chicken mutators as plentiful and as much of a nuisance as mobile phones and laptops are now, the spotlight of public attention and funds will inevitably shift to consumer applications. Where will that leave the ranks of theoretical research biologists amassed by universities? My advice is to think now about saving the job prospects of future generations of academics by reconsidering and expanding the entire subject. That's where I humbly suggest toaster fish come in, and where mathematicians can provide some friendly help. A toaster fish is, of course, a creature with the head of a toaster—the pop-up kind—and the body of a fish. Thus, Figure 1 shows a typical toaster fish. And here is Figure 1. I hope you can see that down there, an artist's representation of a toaster fish. Toaster fish come in at least two different varieties: the North American kind, which operates on 120 volts, and the European kind, which uses 220 volts. Already, I hear a lot of you asking the obvious question: Can North American and European toaster fish mate? This is typical of the important new research problems in the brave new world of biology I'm proposing. Biology needs to embrace the study of life in all its possibilities. There is no need to restrict attention to those creatures actually living or having lived or with the potential of living on planet Earth and its immediate environs. These creatures are but a happy cross-section of all possible life forms. By widening our consideration to arbitrary creatures, we can formulate new theories, create intellectual challenges, unleash our wildest imagination, and, best of all, create jobs. Let's call this emerging field "Life Theory." Toaster fish, baby universes, and slimy galactic super-octopi will all be embraced by this farsighted new intellectual endeavor. But it won't be easy. There'll be colleagues who ridicule life theory—those who call it a theory about nothing or quasi-religious speculation on things that don't exist. Already, one of my friends protests that Figure 1 amounts to little more than a poetic idea since I have not specified the internal workings of the toaster fish depicted. Fortunately, mathematicians have already encountered and triumphed over similar obstacles in their historic struggle to place real numbers squarely at the center of all modern mathematical discourse. The modern biologist can gain from a careful study of this important achievement, which we turn to now with an eye to the main principles. A Short History of Numbers and the Importance of Terminology The Neanderthals gave us one, two, and three. The ancients added on four, five, six, and so on, discovered zero and negative numbers, and employed rationals like 22/3. They discovered that other useful quantities like √2 and π were not of this form and could only be specified by algorithms, which subsequently allowed writing them in binary expansions like √2 = 1.0110101... and π = 11.0010010.... The Europeans added some more useful numbers: complex numbers like 5 + 2i and infinitesimals like 1 + 3dx. It wasn't clear exactly what these numbers were, but their use in the calculus was undisputed, so they remained—until the 19th century, that is, when new standards of rigor revealed that an infinitely small quantity was only a grammatical construction bereft of any real mathematical meaning. So infinitesimals were abandoned, but complex numbers were justified, and constructible irrationals abounded. So we still had numbers galore for every conceivable problem. Then came the greatest, the boldest leap of all: we introduced numbers so abstract that they couldn't possibly be needed for any concrete problem. How? We considered binary expansions that couldn't be constructed by definition. Although this may sound contradictory, it is not. Just consider a slimy galactic super-octopus with an infinitely large brain, an infinite number of arms, and an inclination to wave them all simultaneously and independently either up or down. Looking at the configuration of arms at some random time gives a generally unconstructible sequence. As an aside, I mention that such a creature would be a useful friend, being able to solve the Goldbach conjecture or the Riemann hypothesis simply by computation—and in an infinitesimally small amount of time to boot! More mundanely, an uncomputable binary expansion is simply a binary expansion that is not computable. It does seem curious that such a simple idea eluded all the great thinkers until the latter half of the 19th century. To envision such a number, we proceed as follows: randomly write down a string of zeros and ones—a dozen usually suffices, but if the audience is skeptical, I like to include more—and then add three dots. Thus, α = 1.0110101111001... shows a typical unconstructible number. These new objects, when added onto the collection of rationals and constructible irrationals, gave us a whole new domain of mathematical discourse. Now, a key point: What did we call this new number system, consisting largely of numerical creations of our imagination? Did we call them arbitrary numbers or way-out numbers? No. In a flash of brilliance any PR person would be proud of, we called them real numbers and designated them by the solid-looking ℝ. This inspired terminology is worth emulating. I suggest you call the new area of modern biology concerned with toaster fish, refrigerator fish, and so on the field of "Real Fish." Those real fish actually found in the solar system will then have a special designation, subtly reinforcing the idea that it is unnatural to consider them separately—let's say "Observable Real Fish." How much more of a bother it is to consider the latter! Let α be an observable real fish. You begin your lecture—where is it from? Is it green? What does it eat? Someone immediately asks. Therefore, preferable: Let α be a real fish. This protects you from such unnecessary questions, which you can't answer anyway. In mathematics, restriction to computable real numbers is a decided nuisance. First, one must worry about what computable means: which machine, which language? About whether or not programs halt? Simple operations like adding or multiplying two numbers give us headaches. Real numbers free us from these hassles, which we prefer to leave to the computer scientists anyway. Training the Young How do we get future generations to take the validity of real numbers for granted? We indoctrinate them early in their careers when they are eager but impressionable undergraduates. Here's how we do it: First, we soften them up with a "constructing the real numbers" blurb in their first calculus course. Needless to say, we don't really construct real numbers, as they are by definition unconstructible, but the phrase sticks in their minds long after the details are forgotten. Sometime later, we expose them to the definition of a one-to-one correspondence, Cantor's wonderful proof that there is no list of all binary sequences, and voila! There's a bit of hand-waving about what a set actually is, and a vast and glittering new universe of ordinals, cardinals, hierarchies of infinities, and continuum hypotheses opens up before their eyes. The confusion and apprehension of millennia cast aside, our students are soon fearlessly manipulating arbitrary unions of arbitrarily many powers of uncountable ordinals. It's a heady experience, this first grasping hold of infinity. But be warned: There will be some students who won't buy this. After all, young people are a tad critical at this age. We explain to them that if they want to get into foundational questions, then they had better first master the predicate calculus of first-order logic. Having barely mastered the ordinary calculus, they seem to find this a convincing argument. A further point: We don't start with younger students, since they get too easily confused. They want to know specifics like, how does one add or multiply real numbers? In fact, one can't add or multiply real numbers. But of course, this shouldn't and doesn't prevent us from using these operations on a daily basis. Immature learners are likely to get stuck by these subtleties. The snag is the following: Suppose we want to add the unconstructible real numbers 1.01010101001... and 1.1000100111001.... Clearly, the first digits of the sum are 10.11, but the next digit requires more information. How much more information? Possibly just one more digit, possibly a trillion more digits, and here's the rub—possibly infinitely many more digits. The complementary pattern of zeros and ones might continue to infinity, but if so, we'll never know it. This prevents us from ever specifying a procedure to add real numbers. One might hope that trying some different definition of the reals, using continued fractions or Dedekind cuts or Cauchy sequences of rationals, might solve the problem—but it doesn't. It just transforms it into an equivalent one. But here's where religion—or modern biology—comes to our aid. It may be impossible for us to know whether a given complementary pattern of zeros and ones continues indefinitely, but it is clearly not impossible for God or a slimy intergalactic super-octopus to know. So we are blessedly free to go on talking about adding arbitrary real numbers. Convincing the Professorial This is a question primarily of critical mass. Once enough of the academy accepts and profits by the new bounds of the discipline, a critical momentum will prevent opponents from stemming the tide. First of all, a leading luminary must be taken on board. In our case, that was David Hilbert, whose oft-quoted "No one shall expel us from the paradise that Cantor has created for us" becomes our motto. Furthermore, we must convince our colleagues that opportunities for research are rife. In mathematics, the kind of free thinking that allows the pleasant contemplation of real numbers—I like to think of them as basking in the sun—also revels in more complicated abstractions. Unlike earlier, more squeamish generations, we are quite comfortable with arbitrary functions from ℝ to ℝ—not just those given by formulas or computer programs, but all the others too. With arbitrary operators on spaces of such functions, with arbitrary functionals on algebras of such operators, and so on. Non-measurable functions, Banach-Tarski paradoxes, various versions of the axiom of choice, inaccessible cardinals—none of these ruffles our calm in the brave new world of 20th-century mathematics. More than just jobs are opened up here, though. Our easy attitude to sets can be extended to other mathematical objects too. This liberal approach is captured by what we might call the Axiom of Freedom: An object is constructed or specified—same thing—by simply stating it. To give it one of a multitude of possible uses of this principle: Suppose you finally constructed a quasi-barreled inverted amorphous fiber scheme S, and that all that remains is to exhibit the space P of all coherent quadratic forms from S to its dual. In years gone by, this would have required fretting about precise definitions and concrete realizations. These days, you need only say, "Let P be the space of all coherent quadratic functions from S to its dual." Voila, the construction is complete. Setbacks and Recovery At the turn of the century, with mathematicians divided in controversy over the validity and meaning of Cantor's new theory and the role of real numbers, disaster struck. In one of the most embarrassing intellectual deflations of all time, the entire new theory came crashing down with the discovery of fundamental new paradoxes. These weren't just the niggling kind of inconsistencies acknowledged from the start even by Cantor—they were the full-blown π = √2 type of contradictions that ruin one's entire week. A dark time indeed, but ultimately only a minor setback. After some hesitant and unconvincing attempts to shore up the subject, we now see that worrying about such issues is largely a waste of time. Foundations are not really all that necessary. If pressed by philosophical types, we have a fallback position: mathematics is based on axioms, and the axioms for set theory were spelled out by Zermelo and Fraenkel. This is the foremost position, also pushed by Hilbert, which got rather badly bruised by Gödel's results in the 1930s. As a result, most of us closeted Platonists aren't so keen on this official explanation. But who really wants to get embroiled in philosophical hair-splittings? An intellectual discipline reflects the spirit and the values of the times, and these are times of pragmatism and expediency. If pressed by our own kind—by mathematicians who try to point out the imprecision of our cherished fundamental concepts—we respond politely, we allow them their say, listen patiently, and point out that they're basically only repeating Brouwer's critique of 1918. And what about those who obstinately refuse to understand, who keep on insisting that you explain what exactly is a real fish? Sigh. These people are quibblers, philosophical quibblers at that, and they are best ignored. After all, you've got a job to do. Looking to the Future As pragmatism and expediency don't look to be departing the modern world anytime soon, I'm confident that the lessons mathematicians have learned can be fruitfully applied to biology. Don't expect to convince your contemporaries about the importance of real fish. Recruit a few top names, work on the future generations, keep the employment prospects firmly in view, and life theory will take its rightful place in the scientific world. As for me, this little advice-giving has got me thinking. Mathematicians are themselves a bit hard-pressed these days for employment. The huge amount of time and effort required to rewrite modern mathematics using only computable numbers, computable functions, computable everything would keep us busy for decades. Unpleasant, a lot of hard work that would be, but hard work means jobs, grants, and pensions. Maybe Aristotle, Archimedes, Newton, Euler, Gauss, Kronecker, Borel, Lebesgue, Poincaré, Weyl, Brouwer, and the others were right—there is something fishy about real numbers. Alright, so that's my little article called Real Fish, Real Numbers, Real Jobs, which I published in 1999 in The Mathematical Intelligencer. So you might want to know what kind of response I've gotten. How many dozen citations has this lovely and illuminating paper received? It's received, as far as I know, one citation, and I have gotten correspondence from, I think, one or two people concerning it. I once had a roommate at a conference in New Zealand from Belgium, and we were talking about real numbers, and it turned out that he had read an article some time back about somebody who was also adopting a heretical position. It turned out to be my article, so I was a little bit amused by that—that at least people have read the article and maybe not responded directly to it. But the point is that a single voice is easily ignored. People have a lot of incentive to keep on thinking the same way, to keep on going in the same direction that they used to, which is comfortable and pleasant, and they, of course, are buttressed by the support of a lot of like-thinking colleagues. It's not so easy to change the direction of mathematics. And what I'm going to talk about in my next video is I'm going to address this issue of what mathematics would actually look like if we did seriously contemplate getting rid of real numbers. What would happen? Would it all just collapse? What would mathematics look like without real numbers? I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.
